<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-contiguity Brassica nigra genome localizes active centromeres and defines the ancestral Brassica genome"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2020 6:8"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-08-10"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 Crown"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="It is only recently, with the advent of long-read sequencing technologies, that we are beginning to uncover previously uncharted regions of complex and inherently recursive plant genomes. To comprehensively study and exploit the genome of the neglected oilseed Brassica nigra, we generated two high-quality nanopore de novo genome assemblies. The N50 contig lengths for the two assemblies were 17.1&#8201;Mb (12&#8201;contigs), one of the best among 324&#8201;sequenced plant genomes, and 0.29&#8201;Mb (424&#8201;contigs), respectively, reflecting recent improvements in the technology. Comparison with a de novo short-read assembly corroborated genome integrity and quantified sequence-related error rates (0.2%). The contiguity and coverage allowed unprecedented access to low-complexity regions of the genome. Pericentromeric regions and coincidence of hypomethylation enabled localization of active centromeres and identified centromere-associated ALE family retro-elements that appear to have proliferated through relatively recent nested transposition events (&lt;1&#8201;Ma). Genomic distances calculated based on synteny relationships were used to define a post-triplication Brassica-specific ancestral genome, and to calculate the extensive rearrangements that define the evolutionary distance separating B. nigra from its diploid relatives. Two high-quality nanopore genome assemblies of Brassica nigra are reported, one of which has particularly high contiguity with a contig N50 of 17.1&#8201;Mb, allowing localization of active centromeres and reconstruction of the ancestral Brassica genome."/>

Robinson</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.55614.33, 0000 0001 1302 4958, Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada, Saskatoon, Saskatchewan, Canada" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Sateesh-Kagale" data-author-popup="auth-Sateesh-Kagale">Sateesh Kagale</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-7213-1590"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-7213-1590</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="National href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.55614.33, 0000 0001 1302 4958, Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada, Saskatoon, Saskatchewan, Canada" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation"             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8335-9876"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8335-9876</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of York" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.5685.e, 0000 0004 1936 9668, Department of Biology, University of York, York, UK" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Ian-Bancroft" data-author-popup="auth-Ian-Bancroft">Ian Bancroft</a></span><span class="u-js-hide">

Sharpe<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-1832-4009"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-1832-4009</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Saskatchewan" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.25152.31, 0000 0001 2154 235X, Global Institute for Food Security, University of Saskatchewan, Saskatoon, Saskatchewan, Canada" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Isobel_A__P_-Parkin" data-author-popup="auth-Isobel_A__P_-Parkin" data-corresp-id="c2">Isobel A. P. Parkin<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-5807-9466"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-5807-9466</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.55614.33, 0000 0001 1302 4958, Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada, Saskatoon, Saskatchewan, Canada" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>It is only recently, with the advent of long-read sequencing technologies, that we are beginning to uncover previously uncharted regions of complex and inherently recursive plant genomes. To comprehensively study and exploit the genome of the neglected oilseed <i>Brassica nigra</i>, we generated two high-quality nanopore de novo genome assemblies. The N50 contig lengths for the two assemblies were 17.1 Mb (12 contigs), one of the best among 324 sequenced plant genomes, and 0.29 Mb (424 contigs), respectively, reflecting recent improvements in the technology. Comparison with a de novo short-read assembly corroborated genome integrity and quantified sequence-related error rates (0.2%). The contiguity and coverage allowed unprecedented access to low-complexity regions of the genome. Pericentromeric regions and coincidence of hypomethylation enabled localization of active centromeres and identified centromere-associated ALE family retro-elements that appear to have proliferated through relatively recent nested transposition events (&lt;1 Ma). Genomic distances calculated based on synteny relationships were used to define a post-triplication <i>Brassica-</i>specific ancestral genome, and to calculate the extensive rearrangements that define the evolutionary distance separating <i>B. nigra</i> from its diploid relatives.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Decoding complete genome information is vital for understanding genome structure, providing a full complement of both the genic and repeat repertoire and uncovering structural variation. Such information also provides a foundational tool for crop improvement to facilitate the rapid selection of agronomically important traits and to exploit modern breeding tools such as genome editing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bevan, M. W. et al. Genomic innovation for crop improvement. Nature543, 346–354 (2017)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d44283e671">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Abberton, M. et al. Global agricultural intensification during climate change: a role for genomics. Plant Biotechnol. J.14, 1095–1098 (2016)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d44283e671_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Scheben, A., Wolter, F., Batley, J., Puchta, H. &amp; Edwards, D. Towards CRISPR/Cas crops—bringing together genomics and genome editing. N. Phytol.216, 682–698 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d44283e674">3</a></sup>. Whole-genome duplication and abundant repeat expansion has led to an approximate 660-fold variation in genome size among angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Michael, T. P. Plant genome size variation: bloating and purging DNA. Brief. Funct. Genomics13, 308–317 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d44283e678">4</a></sup> and, in particular, the low complexity of repetitive regions, including centromeric, pericentromeric and telomeric regions, creates challenges for complete genome assembly using short-read (SR) sequence data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lim, K. B. et al. Characterization of the centromere and peri‐centromere retrotransposons in Brassica rapa and their distribution in related Brassica species. Plant J.49, 173–183 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d44283e682">5</a></sup>. Centromeres are of particular interest due to their biological importance, yet resolving their structure has been frustrated by the prevalence of repetitive elements; commonly these are marked by the presence of short, tandemly repeated sequences and, although similar to one other very small plant genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Lan, T. et al. Long-read sequencing uncovers the adaptive topography of a carnivorous plant genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA114, E4435–E4441 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d44283e686">6</a></sup>, no such sequence has been identified for <i>Brassica nigra</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Koo, D. H. et al. Rapid divergence of repetitive DNAs in Brassica relatives. Genomics97, 173–185 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d44283e692">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Muller, H., Gil, J. Jr &amp; Drinnenberg, I. A. J. The impact of centromeres on spatial genome architecture. Trends Genet.35, 565–578 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d44283e695">8</a></sup>.</p><p>Recent advances in long-read (LR) sequencing technologies, such as Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) and Oxford Nanopore Technology (ONT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Sedlazeck, F. J., Lee, H., Darby, C. A. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Piercing the dark matter: bioinformatics of long-range sequencing and mapping. Nat. Rev. Genet.19, 329–346 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44283e702">9</a></sup>, combined with genome scaffolding methods such as optical mapping and chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C), have led to a paradigm shift in our ability to obtain complete and contiguous genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sedlazeck, F. J., Lee, H., Darby, C. A. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Piercing the dark matter: bioinformatics of long-range sequencing and mapping. Nat. Rev. Genet.19, 329–346 (2018)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d44283e706">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, W.-B. &amp; Schneeberger, K. The impact of third generation genomic technologies on plant genome assembly. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol.36, 64–70 (2017)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d44283e706_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Koren, S. &amp; Phillippy, A. M. One chromosome, one contig: complete microbial genomes from long-read sequencing and assembly. Curr. Opin. Microbiol.23, 110–120 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d44283e709">11</a></sup>. Both approaches can produce remarkably long reads, although the error rate is markedly higher than more accurate Illumina short reads, which until recently limited their use to scaffolding in improving assembly contiguity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature546, 524–527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d44283e713">12</a></sup>. However, correction algorithms to reduce error rates and recent technological improvements have increased the output and quality of LR sequence data, making possible the routine and cost-effective assembly of large eukaryotic genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Deamer, D., Akeson, M. &amp; Branton, D. Three decades of nanopore sequencing. Nat. Biotechnol.34, 518–524 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d44283e717">13</a></sup>.</p><p>The Brassicaceae is an important plant family with approximately 3,800 species including commercially important vegetable, fodder, oilseed and ornamental crops. The Brassiceae tribe has a history of extensive whole-genome duplication events, including the <i>Brassica</i> genus-specific whole-genome triplication (WGT), which occurred approximately 22.5 million years ago (Ma) (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kagale, S. et al. Polyploid evolution of the Brassicaceae during the Cenozoic era. Plant Cell26, 2777–2791 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d44283e727">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Lysak, M. A., Koch, M. A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res.15, 516–525 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d44283e730">15</a></sup>) and is assumed to be shared by the three important diploids (<i>Brassica rapa</i>, AA, 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> =20; <i>B. nigra</i>, BB, 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 16; and <i>Brassica oleracea</i>, CC, 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 18) that form the vertices of U’s triangle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Nagaharu, U. &amp; Nagaharu, N. Genome analysis in Brassica with special reference to the experimental formation of B. napus and peculiar mode of fertilization. Jpn. J. Bot.7, 389–452 (1935)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d44283e763">16</a></sup>. Among these three, <i>B. nigra</i> (B genome) has been neglected with regard to both genetic analyses and selection through breeding. Due to its limited domestication and its production as out-crossing populations, it has retained valuable allelic diversity compared to its relatives, making it an untapped repository for <i>Brassica</i> breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Truco, M. J. &amp; Quiros, C. F. Structure and organization of the B genome based on a linkage map in Brassica nigra. Theor. Appl. Genet.89, 590–598 (1994)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d44283e773">17</a></sup>. Among the six species of U’s triangle, five have been sequenced including, most recently, <i>B. nigra</i>, but the assemblies cover at most 80% of the estimated genome size and almost all were very highly fragmented due to the sole use of SR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome Biol.15, R77 (2014)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d44283e780">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun.5, 3930 (2014)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d44283e780_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science345, 950–953 (2014)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d44283e780_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, L. et al. Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Hortic. Res.5, 50 (2018)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d44283e780_3">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Yang, J. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet.48, 1225–1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d44283e783">22</a></sup>. Recently the <i>B. rapa</i> reference genome was improved using PacBio sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, L. et al. Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Hortic. Res.5, 50 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d44283e791">21</a></sup>, and one genotype each of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> was sequenced using a combination of ONT and optical maps, demonstrating the use of these technologies for complex duplicated genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Belser, C. et al. Chromosome-scale assemblies of plant genomes using nanopore long reads and optical maps. Nat. Plants4, 879–887 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d44283e801">23</a></sup>.</p><p>The work described represents the near-complete assembly of two <i>B. nigra</i> genomes (Ni100 and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a>) using a combination of ONT sequencing, Hi-C and genetic map-based scaffolding. A SR assembly of Ni100 allowed comprehensive benchmarking of the LR assemblies. Remarkably, direct methylome profiling using the ONT data allowed the resolution of candidate active centromeres of the chromosomes, a feature previously unannotated in SR assemblies. In addition, computationally defined genomic distances between the three <i>Brassica</i> diploid genomes allowed the construction of an ancestral <i>Brassica-</i>specific genome.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>A combination of nanopore sequencing, Illumina error correction, Hi-C sequencing and genetic mapping was used to generate two de novo assemblies for the diploid <i>Brassica</i> species, <i>B. nigra</i> (genotypes Ni100 and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a>). Identical sequential steps were followed to assemble the contigs for each genome, including the development of high-quality sequencing datasets, genome assembly and polishing with SR (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">1</a>). After testing a number of published assembly software pipelines (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">1</a>), the final contigs were derived from SMARTdenovo using 30–64× coverage of CANU<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Koren, S. et al. CANU: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res.27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d44283e852">24</a></sup>-corrected reads.</p><p>Although largely context dependent, nanopore sequence data can show error rates up to 15%. Thus, sequence correction was completed using eight rounds of Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d44283e859">25</a></sup> with approximately 100× coverage of Illumina data, and quality was assessed at each round through benchmarking universal single-copy orthologue (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d44283e863">26</a></sup> scores and qualimap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Okonechnikov, K., Conesa, A. &amp; García-Alcalde, F. Qualimap 2: advanced multi-sample quality control for high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics32, 292–294 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d44283e867">27</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">2</a>). For both genotypes the read alignment rate was high (&gt;98%) and both tools indicated a significant improvement after correction, suggesting final error rates of between 0.8% (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a>) and 0.2% (Ni100) at the base pair (bp) level. The two LR assemblies were generated over a period of approximately 12 months, during which time ONT upgraded their library construction kits, pore chemistry and base-calling software. The combined impact of this was noted in an overall improvement in quality, average read length and useable data output for Ni100 and in final assembly contiguity (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">3</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">5</a>). Because the <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> assembly was more fragmented (compare a contig N50 length of 0.288 Mb with 17.1 Mb), scaffolding using proximity ligation, a combination of Chicago and Hi-C, was used to improve contiguity by up to 193-fold, with a final N50 length of 55.7 Mb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">3</a>). In both instances genetic anchoring was used to generate the final chromosome-scale assemblies of the two <i>B. nigra</i> genotypes, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> (C2-LR) and Ni100 (Ni100-LR) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Tab1">1</a>, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the <i>B. nigra</i> genome SR and LR assemblies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genomic features of the B. nigra Ni100-LR assembly."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Genomic features of the <i>B. nigra</i> Ni100-LR assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Bands: (1) chromosomes with centromere positions (black band); (2) class I retrotransposons (nucleotides per 100-kb bins); (3) class II DNA repeats (nucleotides per 100-kb bins); (4) gene density (genes per 100-kb bins); (5) gene expression in leaf tissue (log<sub>10</sub>[average TPM] in 100-kb bins); (6) ONT CG methylation profile (ratio per 100 kb); (7) whole-genome bisulfite methylation profile (nucleotides per 100-kb bins). CG, blue; CHG, yellow; CHH, red.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A SR Illumina de novo assembly for <i>B. nigra</i> genotype Ni100 (Ni100-SR) was used for further validation of the nanopore assemblies. The Ni100-SR assembly has a total length of 446.5 Mb from 19,203 scaffolds, of which 367.2 Mb was anchored to eight pseudo-chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">6</a>). Alignment and visualization of corresponding pseudo-chromosome sequences from the three <i>B. nigra</i> assemblies revealed high levels of collinearity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig2">2a</a>). Such high-level comparisons can elucidate large-scale chromosome rearrangements and a number of translocations and inversions were noted—in particular, a large inversion at the bottom of B4 distinguished the SR assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">5</a>). This region on B4 was difficult to scaffold in the SR assembly due to limited recombination and shorter scaffold lengths; for such regions in the SR assembly, the order was largely inferred based on synteny data from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. It was apparent that there was expansion of the ONT assemblies in regions presumed to be pericentromeric, as shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig2">2</a>. The level of coverage of these regions also varied between the LR assemblies, with Ni100-LR having the highest.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparison of B. nigra assemblies."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Comparison of <i>B. nigra</i> assemblies.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="748" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Chromosome-level genome alignment of the Ni100-SR (NS) assembly (centre) against the LR assemblies, C2-LR (bottom) and Ni100-LR (top). The plot was created using Synvisio (<a href="https://github.com/kiranbandi/synvisio">https://github.com/kiranbandi/synvisio</a>). <b>b</b>, Circular map generated using Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res.19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1543">89</a></sup> showing the alignment of the SR and LR assemblies for chromosome B5 of Ni100.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Gene annotation from the two LR and two SR assemblies (Ni100-SR and the previously published YZ12151 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Yang, J. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet.48, 1225–1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1558">22</a></sup>)) were rationalized to generate a final <i>B. nigra</i> gene complement of 67,030 and 59,877 gene models in the two genotypes, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> and Ni100, respectively. These numbers are in line with the predicted pan-gene content of the diploid <i>B. oleracea</i>, with 63,865 ± 31 genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Golicz, A. A. et al. The pangenome of an agronomically important crop plant Brassica oleracea. Nat. Commun.7, 13390 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1575">28</a></sup>. An additional 3,546 genes were annotated in Ni100-LR compared to the Ni100-SR assembly. A homology search performed using GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. J. B. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1580">29</a></sup> (minimum identity and coverage of 95%) indicated that only 914 of the additional genes were unique to the Ni100-LR assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">6a</a>). This discrepancy was due both to co-assembly of highly similar genes in the SR data and assembly errors that precluded accurate gene annotation. Read mapping of Illumina data back to the SR and LR assemblies showed a marked increase of 9% multi-mapping reads in the latter with a concomitant reduction in non-concordant matches, suggesting the resolution of duplicated or highly homologous sequences in the LR assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">7</a>). The recent ONT assemblies of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> studied the self-incompatibility locus or the S locus region which, due to its repetitive structure, has been notoriously difficult to assemble, to infer the enhanced contiguity of the LR-derived genome sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Bachmann, J. A., Tedder, A., Laenen, B., Steige, K. A. &amp; Slotte, T. J. Targeted long-read sequencing of a locus under long-term balancing selection in Capsella. G3 (Bethesda)8, 1327–1333 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1596">30</a></sup>. The S locus region was identified and compared in the two <i>B. nigra</i> LR assemblies, showing complete assembly of two differing S locus haplotypes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">7</a>). A comparison between the two ONT assemblies would have been expected to identify such genotype differences. Along with approximately 10% of the annotated genes being specific to either assembly, the <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> genotype showed a higher prevalence of tandemly and proximally duplicated genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">6c</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">10</a>).</p><p>To investigate global gene content across the genomes, annotated genes from the three representations of <i>B. nigra</i> were clustered with genes from <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> using Orthofinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. J. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol.16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1643">31</a></sup> (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">6d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">8a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">11</a>). The diploid <i>Brassica</i> species ranged in number of species-specific genes, with <i>B. rapa</i> containing the least while <i>B. nigra</i> contained the most (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">8b,c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">11</a>). Sixty-nine of <i>B. nigra-</i>specific gene families were deemed to be rapidly evolving by CAFE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. J. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol.30, 1987–1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1676">32</a></sup>, and functional analyses demonstrated that the genes were enriched in response to abiotic or biotic stresses, structural molecule activity and unknown molecular functions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">9</a>). Since it is often noted that families related to stress are more prone to differential copy number variation, differences in R genes, transcription factors (TFs) and protein-kinase families were assessed in each of the genomes. The distribution of R gene families across the species appeared to be directly related to genome size and/or expansion of the transposable element complement, with the larger genomes of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. nigra</i> showing the greatest expansion of R genes irrespective of genotype (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">12</a>). <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> in particular showed increased membership of TF families, with both <i>B. nigra</i> genotypes showing a higher prevalence of B3, C2H2 and NAC domain TFs compared to their diploid relatives (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">14</a>).</p><p>Beyond the large chromosomal rearrangements, structural variations (SVs) in the range of 100 bp to a few Mb, including deletions, insertions, duplications, inversions and translocations that differentiate genotypes, were catalogued between both genomes using ONT reads. The raw ONT reads from Ni100 and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> were aligned to both LR assemblies, and SVs were quantified using two different SV callers (Sniffles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Sedlazeck, F. J. et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat. Methods15, 461–468 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1719">33</a></sup> and Picky<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gong, L. et al. Picky comprehensively detects high-resolution structural variants in nanopore long reads. Nat. Methods15, 455–460 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1723">34</a></sup>). Self-alignment was used to estimate a false-positive rate for each genome, which was higher for the <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> assembly (6,307 versus 2,230 events) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">15</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">10</a>). High-quality SVs were considered to be those identified with both software packages (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">15</a>). Dependent on direction of comparison, approximately 6,000–7,000 SVs differentiated the two genotypes, with deletions being the most prevalent (between 63.4 and 70% of events). A small number of deletions were coincident with gene annotations, affecting between 865 and 638 genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">16</a>), while a notable proportion was found proximal to genes and thus might have been anticipated to impact expression and/or gene copy number (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">10b,c</a>). A set of 161 and 136 SVs in the two genomes were found to overlap completely with annotated full-length, functional, transposable elements, suggesting a mechanism for their formation (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">17</a>).</p><p>A <i>Brassica</i> B genome-specific repeat library with 1,324 families was developed using multiple annotation tools and was used to survey the repetitive genome fraction of the LR (Ni100-LR, C2-LR) and SR <i>B. nigra</i> assemblies (Ni100-SR and YZ12151-SR) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">18</a>). Repeats spanned 49 and 54% of the <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> and Ni100-LR genome assemblies, respectively, compared to 33% (YZ12151) and 41% (Ni100) in the two SR assemblies. The increase in repeat content of the LR assemblies, which closely mirrors the increase in genome captured, predominantly resulted from a rise in annotated class I transposons, in particular Gypsy and Copia elements, which increased by 8% (79 versus 130 Mb) and 4.1% (26 versus 51 Mb), respectively, in the Ni100-LR assembly compared to the Ni100-SR assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">18</a>). The distribution of repeats revealed that class I retrotransposons were more common in traditionally heterochromatic regions such as centromeric, pericentromeric and subtelomeric regions, while class II DNA transposons were more evenly distributed across the genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">4</a>). The identification of centromere- and telomere-specific repeats suggested that ONT assemblies provide more complete access to the chromosome structure (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">11</a>). The repeat fraction appears to reflect the estimated genome size of the diploid <i>Brassica</i>s, with <i>B. nigra</i> lying between <i>B. oleracea</i> (~60%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Belser, C. et al. Chromosome-scale assemblies of plant genomes using nanopore long reads and optical maps. Nat. Plants4, 879–887 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1795">23</a></sup> and <i>B. rapa</i> (~38%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, L. et al. Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Hortic. Res.5, 50 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1802">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Belser, C. et al. Chromosome-scale assemblies of plant genomes using nanopore long reads and optical maps. Nat. Plants4, 879–887 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1805">23</a></sup>.</p><p>Almost all families were similarly distributed in the two LR assemblies apart from LTR-Gypsy elements, which were ~5% higher in Ni100, suggesting either Ni100-specific amplification or better assembly of these elements (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">18</a>). Full-length long terminal repeat retrotransposons (FL-LTR-RTs) were annotated and compared in Ni100-SR and the two LR assemblies. A total of 1,220, 4,491 and 3,381 FL-LTR-RTs were identified in Ni100-SR, Ni100-LR and C2-LR assemblies, respectively, with an average length of ~6 kb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">19</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">12a</a>). The increased annotation of such elements in the LR assemblies indicates the benefits of the technology in regard to assemblage of low-complexity, redundant sequences. Based on repeat domain protein homology, the FL-LTR-RTs were grouped into 14 different families where 41–44% had homology with known Gypsy families, 38–42% with Copia families and 13–20% were unknown FL-LTR-RTs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig3">3a</a>). Notably, among the 14 FL-LTR-RT families, members of the ALE (Copia) and OTA (Gypsy) families were specifically increased in copy number in the LR assemblies and more so in the Ni100-LR assembly (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">19</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">12a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Annotation of FL-LTR-RTs in B. nigra genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Annotation of FL-LTR-RTs in <i>B. nigra</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="965" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Copy number of FL-LTR-RTs from 14 different families. <b>b</b>, Age distribution of FL-LTR-RTs in three <i>B. nigra</i> assemblies. <b>c</b>, Comparison of insertion sites of two FL-LTR-RTs (ALE and OTA) in the ONT assemblies.</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM4">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The age distribution analysis of FL-LTR-RT elements, based on divergence of the LTR region, showed recent amplification of LTRs in both genomes. About 91% (4,068) and 86% (2,912) of FL-LTR-RTs in Ni100-LR and C2-LR assemblies, respectively, were amplified &lt;2 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig3">3b</a>, Supplementary Figs <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">12b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">13</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">19</a>), with more recent and continuous proliferation of LTRs (3,056, 68%) aged &lt;1 Ma in Ni100-LR compared to C2-LR. ALE family elements showed more recent amplification (&lt;0.2 Ma) in Ni100-LR compared to C2-LR, while OTA LTRs showed a more consistent pattern between the two genomes. Analysis of the insertion sites revealed that 405 OTA (59%) and 391 ALE (42%) were conserved in both genomes and, in line with the increase in density, there was a higher percentage of uniquely inserted ALE elements in the Ni100-LR genome compared to the C2-LR genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig3">3c</a>). A phylogenetic analysis of ALE and OTA FL-LTRs suggested Ni100 genotype-specific amplification of particular members of each family (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><p>Oxford Nanopore Technology allows direct identification of base modifications such as 5-methylcytosine (5-mC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Simpson, J. T. et al. Detecting DNA cytosine methylation using nanopore sequencing. Nat. Methods14, 407–410 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1898">35</a></sup>, although this has yet to be demonstrated in plant genomes. Nanopolish was used to detect 5-mC in the CG context in the ONT unassembled reads. The 5-mC calls for Ni100 had an area under the curve score of 0.9, with calls made at 58% of sites using the default threshold of 2.5 for the log-likelihood ratio (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">15</a>). The resultant calls were compared with methylation status detected using whole-genome bisulfite sequence (WGBS) data, and showed excellent correlation irrespective of filtering for quality of call (<i>R</i> = 0.93–0.97; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig4">4c–e</a>). A comparison of C2 genome methylation frequencies generated using the two methods showed a slightly lower correlation (0.68–0.80), suggesting that raw read error rate and sequence depth play a crucial role in analysis of methylation using ONT reads (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">16</a>). As perhaps expected, the observed methylation showed patterns similar to those detected for related <i>Brassica</i> diploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome Biol.15, R77 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d44283e1918">18</a></sup>, with a higher prevalence of 5-mC in repeat sequences and lower methylation rates across annotated gene bodies (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">17</a>). The efficacy of ONT calls in the biological context of gene proximity mirrored the pattern observed for the WGBS data, where methylation increases at the transcriptional start and stop sites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig4">4a</a>). Because Nanopolish employs short <i>k</i>-mers in its strategy to make a call, this could impact physically linked calls; however, a comparison of the two approaches to identifying differentially methylated CG islands was in agreement, with some variance in the individual site calls within the island (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Comparison of methylation data from WGBS and ONT sequencing in Ni100."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Comparison of methylation data from WGBS and ONT sequencing in Ni100.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="629" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Genome-wide WGBS and ONT methylation profile of syntenic genes: CpG (purple), CHG (green), CHH (grey) and CpG by ONT (red). <b>b</b>, Genome features of the B2 chromosome of the Ni100-LR assembly, from outer to inner circle: gene density, class II DNA transposons, class I retrotransposons, chromosome cartoon, methylation profile from ONT data, methylation profile based on WGBS, ALE copia, CRB, <i>B. nigra-</i>specific centromeric tandem repeat, putative centromere region. This plot was developed using the AccuSyn tool (<a href="https://accusyn.usask.ca/">https://accusyn.usask.ca/</a>). <b>c</b>–<b>e</b>, Comparison of 5-mC frequency detected by WGBS and ONT; frequency distribution plot without filtering (<b>c</b>) and with filtering based on either calls <i>P</i> ≤ 0.05 (<b>d</b>) or minimum (min.) ONT read depth of 10 (<b>e</b>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Of note, there were regions of reduced methylation observed for each chromosome that were also associated with regions of lower gene and higher repeat density. Centromeric regions have been associated in <i>Brassica</i> species and, more specifically in <i>B. nigra</i>, with particular sequences including centromeric retrotransposon of <i>Brassica</i> (CRB) and a B genome-specific short repeat fragment (pBN 35)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Koo, D. H. et al. Rapid divergence of repetitive DNAs in Brassica relatives. Genomics97, 173–185 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2005">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Lim, K. B. et al. Characterization of the centromere and peri-centromere retrotransposons in Brassica rapa and their distribution in related Brassica species. Plant J.49, 173–183 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2008">36</a></sup>. The distribution of these centromere-associated repeats aligned with the detected hypomethylated regions. Furthermore, members of the more prevalent ALE family, which also has &gt;70% homology with CRB, localized to the same region (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">19</a>). More recently, sequences identified through interaction with the centromere-specific histone, CENH3, have been sequenced for <i>B. nigra</i>, which co-aligned with the hypomethylated regions, suggesting capture of much of the active centromere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wang, G.-X. et al. ChIP-cloning analysis uncovers centromere-specific retrotransposons in Brassica nigra and reveals their rapid diversification in Brassica allotetraploids. Chromosoma128, 119–131 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2022">37</a></sup>.</p><p>Although analyses of nested LTRs have generally been limited to cereal genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kronmiller, B. A. &amp; Wise, R. P. TEnest: automated chronological annotation and visualization of nested plant transposable elements. Plant Physiol.146, 45–59 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2029">38</a></sup>, they would be expected to play a major role in the evolution of chromosome structure and, specifically, in repeat-dense regions such as centromeres. ALE-LTRs were prominent in the centromeric regions and revealed high levels of nested insertion (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig5">5</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">20</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">21</a>). Overall, 262 nested transposable element events were found throughout the Ni100-LR genome of which 68% (179) were in centromeric regions. Across all events, most involved two LTRs while ten events involved more than two LTRs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig5">5b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">21</a>). In-depth characterization of nested TEs in the centromeric region of chromosome B5 revealed that 24/26 of nested transposable element events were created by ALE-LTRs, and all bar one of the events involved the same family member inserting into the host LTR (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig5">5</a>). The predominantly young age (&lt;1 Ma) of the nested elements suggests continuous and recent rearrangement of the centromeric regions by this mechanism (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig5">5b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Characterization of centromeric region of chromosome B5 of Ni100-LR genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Characterization of centromeric region of chromosome B5 of Ni100-LR genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="341" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of various genomic features on the 5-Mb centromere region, including genes, methylome (ONT and WGBS) and full-length LTRs (ALE-LTR and 13 other family LTRs); distribution of young (&lt;1 Ma) and old LTRs (&gt;1 Ma); and distribution of centromeric repeat sequences of <i>B. nigra</i> based on chromatin immunoprecipitation (ChIP) analysis of CENH3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kronmiller, B. A. &amp; Wise, R. P. TEnest: automated chronological annotation and visualization of nested plant transposable elements. Plant Physiol.146, 45–59 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2072">38</a></sup>). <b>b</b>, Nested insertion of full-length LTRs in the centromeric region. Age (in Ma) is shown above each element.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Much effort has been placed on defining an ancestral Crucifer genome that predates the supposed <i>Brassica-</i>specific WGT event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lysak, M. A., Mandáková, T. &amp; Schranz, M. E. J. Coipb Comparative paleogenomics of crucifers: ancestral genomic blocks revisited. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol.30, 108–115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2093">39</a></sup>. Ancestral karyotype blocks were constructed for C2-LR and Ni100-LR based on shared gene content and order for orthologous copies of each <i>A. thaliana</i> gene (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">20</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">9</a>). Based on the two-step mode of genome evolution inferred from the genomes of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Cheng, F. et al. Biased gene fractionation and dominant gene expression among the subgenomes of Brassica rapa. PLoS ONE7, e36442 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2112">40</a></sup>, which is predicated on genome dominance in newly formed polyploids, as expected the blocks were found predominantly in three copies but with biased genic content. The least fractionated genome maintains approximately 70% of the orthologous gene copies, while the most fractionated, 1 (MF1) and MF2, retain approximately 49 and 42%, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">6a</a>). A phylogenetic analysis of the triplicated orthologues confirmed a shared WGT among the <i>Brassica</i>s, with genes from across the three species of each triplicated genome being more similar than those within the same species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig6">6c</a>). Some smaller genomic regions were found in additional syntenic blocks in each genome, which could represent more ancient whole-genome duplication events or further localized segmental translocations. These supplementary blocks were more prevalent in the <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> genome and could explain the higher prevalence of duplicated genes in this genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">6c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Genome rearrangements and evolution of Brassica species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Genome rearrangements and evolution of <i>Brassica</i> species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0735-y/MediaObjects/41477_2020_735_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="622" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Development of <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. nigra</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> genomes based on ancestral genome. Blocks are ‘painted’ with colours corresponding to ancestral chromosomes. <b>b</b>, Divergence time estimation based on Ks distributions. Gaussian mixture models fitted to frequency distributions of Ks values obtained by comparing pairs of syntelogs between different <i>Brassica</i> species or the subgenomes of each species are shown. <b>c</b>, Phylogenetic relationship between the subgenomes of different <i>Brassica</i> species. A maximum-likelihood tree constructed based on concatenated sequences of 1,150 syntelogs between <i>A. thaliana</i> and each of the subgenomes (LF, MF1 and MF2) of <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. nigra</i> is presented. Clade support values near nodes represent bootstrap proportions in percentages. All unmarked nodes have absolute support.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Genomic differences or similarities among species, as well as the mechanisms by which genomes evolve, can be identified by comparing the order in which genes or syntenic blocks appear in both close and distant relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Eichler, E. E. &amp; Sankoff, D. J. Structural dynamics of eukaryotic chromosome evolution. Science301, 793–797 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2202">41</a></sup>. The changes to block orders are defined in terms of certain rearrangement operations within a chromosome or between chromosomes, such as reversal, transposition, fusion, fission and translocations. These types of operation can be abstracted computationally as a series that results in a change in the linear ordering of genes, which can then be used to calculate the ‘genomic distance’ between one version of a genome and another based on the most parsimonious evolutionary process. The double-cut-and-join (DCJ) model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Yancopoulos, S., Attie, O. &amp; Friedberg, R. J. B. Efficient sorting of genomic permutations by translocation, inversion and block interchange. Bioinformatics21, 3340–3346 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2206">42</a></sup> was used to calculate pairwise genomic distances between the three <i>Brassica</i> diploid genomes: <i>d</i><sub><i>rapa,nigra</i></sub> = 96; <i>d</i><sub><i>nigra,oleracea</i></sub> = 98; <i>d</i><sub><i>rapa,oleracea</i></sub> = 52. In addition to measurement of genomic difference or similarity, the order of blocks in extant genomes provides rich information that can be used in reconstruction of ancestral gene orders.</p><p>The ancestral <i>Brassica</i> genome, which minimizes the number of rearrangements and thus genomic distances between the three genomes, has nine ancestral chromosomes with a genome size of 321 Mb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">21</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">22</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">23</a>), consisting of 178 blocks. Each block in the ancestral genome was mapped to the three extant genomes, as shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig6">6a</a>, while Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">22</a> shows the detailed position of each ancestral block and its relative orientation. Based on calculated genomic distances between the ancestral genome and each diploid, a rooted ultrametric phylogenetic tree was approximated (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig6">6a</a>) where the position of the ancestor minimizes the total genomic distance. Based on the molecular clock hypothesis, which assumes a constant rate of change within lineages, the ancestor would be the most recent common ancestor of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> while an overall ancestor would be inferred almost 1/3 of the way along a path from the median to <i>B. nigra</i>.
