The genomic distance between the genomes corresponded with the age of divergence estimated from the synonymous substitutions (Ks) rates among the coding regions of orthologous gene pairs across the genomes, with <i>B. oleracea</i>/<i>B. rapa</i> having diverged from <i>B. nigra</i> some 11.5 Ma while they diverged from each other only 6.8 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig6">6b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">24</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Recent advancements and cost reductions in LR sequencing technologies are facilitating the generation of high-quality genome assemblies, even for species that have evolved through recursive whole-genome duplication (WGD) events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Michael, T. P. et al. High contiguity Arabidopsis thaliana genome assembly with a single nanopore flow cell. Nat. Commun.9, 541 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2290">43</a></sup>. High-quality and highly contiguous assemblies were generated for two genotypes of the mesopolyploid <i>B. nigra</i> using nanopore sequencing, chromosome-level scaffolding with Hi-C and genetic mapping data. Remarkably, the final contig N50 length was 17.1 Mb (Ni100-LR), one of the longest among the 324 plant genomes published to date (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">23</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">25</a>). Comparing the two ONT assemblies, the Ni100-LR assembly was better in terms of contiguity and capture of repeat-rich centromeric regions, reflecting rapid improvements in the technology and suggesting the importance of both read length (11 versus 20 kb) and read coverage (29 versus 64×). Accurate quantification of errors in the Ni100 nanopore assembly, by comparison with an Illumina SR assembly of the same genotype, suggested an accuracy of 99.986%, which was improved only marginally (99.998%) with eight rounds of SR polishing, suggesting that nanopore reads can provide highly accurate assemblies of complex genomes. The error rate was higher for the <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> assembly (0.8 versus 0.2%), again reflecting recent improvements in ONT technology. The determined error rate may also be impacted by genome complexity, since matching of Illumina reads to regions of low complexity is generally limiting, and thus it might be expected that error rates will be higher in such regions.</p><p>The recognition that both small (copy number, presence/absence) and large (chromosomal rearrangements) SVs play an important role in controlling key agronomic traits is gaining traction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Gabur, I., Chawla, H. S., Snowdon, R. J. &amp; Parkin, I. A. P. Connecting genome structural variation with complex traits in crop plants. Theor. Appl. Genet.132, 733–750 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2313">44</a></sup>, yet deciphering such variation with SR data has proved problematic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Cameron, D. L., Di Stefano, L. &amp; Papenfuss, A. T. Comprehensive evaluation and characterisation of short read general-purpose structural variant calling software. Nat. Commun.10, 3240 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2317">45</a></sup>. Although long-read sequencing technologies have distinct advantages in predicting SVs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="De Coster, W. et al. Structural variants identified by Oxford Nanopore PromethION sequencing of the human genome. Genome Res.29, 1178–1187 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2321">46</a></sup>, the current limitation is in regard to developing and training software to accurately identify such variants. The current analyses provided a detailed picture of large-scale rearrangements that differentiate genotypes, and also used two widely accepted software tools for detection of SVs, and cross-validation was attempted to improve the accuracy of the calls. The large difference in the number of events discovered by the different protocols probably reflects a combination of a higher false and a lower positive discovery rate between the two. Considering only the cross-validated calls, a large number of events differentiated the two <i>B. nigra</i> genotypes, many of which would impact gene expression and potentially phenotype, thus underlying the need for improved tools for SV analyses to capture this valuable information.</p><p>It is well established that LR sequence data provide a more comprehensive coverage of the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="van Dijk, E. L., Jaszczyszyn, Y., Naquin, D. &amp; Thermes, C. The third revolution in sequencing technology. Trends Genet.34, 666–681 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2331">47</a></sup>, perhaps most obviously reflected in the increased capture of low-complexity repeat sequences. Repeat analysis revealed about 14% more repeats in the LR assembly of Ni100 compared to the SR assembly (54 versus 41%) and, in particular, a more complete assembly of the repeat-rich centromeric and pericentromeric space. Centromeres are structures essential for the maintenance of karyotype integrity during meiosis, ensuring the fertility of developed gametes through strict inheritance of full chromosome complements; nevertheless, centromeres still remain under-explored, especially in larger genomes. Although the active centromere is incredibly diverse in size and sequence among species, it is characterized through its cohesion with the centromere-specific histone H3-like protein, CENH3, and it has been suggested that association with CENH3 is controlled through epigenetic means, including a decrease in CG methylation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zhang, W., Lee, H.-R., Koo, D.-H. &amp; Jiang, J. Epigenetic modification of centromeric chromatin: hypomethylation of DNA sequences in the CENH3-associated chromatin in Arabidopsis thaliana and maize. Plant Cell20, 25–34 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2335">48</a></sup>. Direct CG methylation profiling using the ONT data not only suggested the efficacy of this approach (93–97% correlation with WGBS) but also demarcated the active centromere in the assembly, with hypomethylated regions being co-located with known and new centromeric repeat sequences. At least three of the chromosomes for Ni100 (B1, B3 and B8) showed multiple hypomethylated islands within or adjacent to the putative centromere region, which also coincided with centromeric-specific repeats (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">24</a>). It was noted for <i>B. rapa</i> that such repeats found outside the presumed centromeric region may represent evidence of ancient palaeo-centromeres, remnants of WGD events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, L. et al. Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Hortic. Res.5, 50 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2345">21</a></sup>. However, all additional sites coincided with hypomethylation, suggesting functionality of the regions. This could imply potential scaffolding errors remaining in the dense repeat regions although, interestingly, even though the data were more limiting the same pattern appeared to be apparent for the <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> genotype, which could suggest a dispersed structure for the active centromeric region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Muller, H., Gil, J. Jr &amp; Drinnenberg, I. A. J. The impact of centromeres on spatial genome architecture. Trends Genet.35, 565–578 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2357">8</a></sup>. Where comparison was feasible, the two genotypes showed a common dichotomy of centromeric regions, with conservation of gene content but rapid divergence in sequence constitution driven by changes in retrotransposon composition.</p><p>Recent work in <i>B. nigra</i> to uncover centromere-specific sequences through their association with CENH3 indicated that, unlike its diploid relatives and almost all analysed plant genomes, <i>B. nigra</i> contains no tandemly repeated satellite DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Lan, T. et al. Long-read sequencing uncovers the adaptive topography of a carnivorous plant genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA114, E4435–E4441 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2370">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wang, G.-X. et al. ChIP-cloning analysis uncovers centromere-specific retrotransposons in Brassica nigra and reveals their rapid diversification in Brassica allotetraploids. Chromosoma128, 119–131 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2373">37</a></sup>. Similarly, although no characteristic tandem repeat was found in the LR assemblies, analyses of assembled full-length LTRs revealed recently amplified (&lt; 1 Ma) elements, in particular ALE-LTRs in the Ni100-LR genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig5">5</a>). Notably all eight of the centromeric regions displayed a very similar structure with a core region largely populated with ALE elements flanked by dense islands of the previously described pBN 35 short repetitive element (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">11</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">20</a>). Interestingly a number of the retro-elements encompassed the pBN 35 sequence within their LTR domains, which might suggest its capture during element activity. Rapid amplification of young LTRs in a nested insertion fashion was observed in all Ni100 centromeric regions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">21</a>), a phenomenon that was not obvious for the available LR assembly of <i>B. rapa</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">26</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, L. et al. Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Hortic. Res.5, 50 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2396">21</a></sup>. Nested TE insertion is a prevalent phenomenon among monocots, but has been identified only infrequently among dicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Kronmiller, B. A. &amp; Wise, R. P. J. Computational finishing of large sequence contigs reveals interspersed nested repeats and gene islands in the rf1-associated region of maize. Plant Physiol.151, 483–495 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2400">49</a></sup>. The detected recent nested insertion events involving a single family suggest that ALE or related LTRs might play an important role in the rapid divergence of centromeres in <i>B. nigra</i>, similar to that found when comparing the centromeric region of two rice genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Gao, D., Jiang, N., Wing, R. A., Jiang, J. &amp; Jackson, S. A. Transposons play an important role in the evolution and diversification of centromeres among closely related species. Front. Plant Sci.6, 216 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2408">50</a></sup>. It was postulated that retrotransposons are actively recruited to the functional centromere; &gt;90% of the <i>B. nigra</i> sequences found to be associated with CENH3 showed significant homology to ALE elements, providing circumstantial evidence for the role of CENH3 in their accumulation at the centromere core (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">27</a>). Further studies are required to fully establish the role of these elements in centromere function in <i>B. nigra</i> and, indeed, the LR assembly resources developed for the Ni100 genotype could be leveraged as a model for centromere function research in future.</p><p>Although an ancestral block structure was established for <i>Brassica</i>s some years ago, it was largely defined manually and there has been no clear resolution of the events separating genomes. Syntenic relationships between species have been instrumental in gene discovery and in dissecting genome evolution that can impact technology transfer across species. The improved assemblies for all three diploid <i>Brassica</i> genomes allowed an ancestral <i>Brassica</i> genome (<i>n</i> = 9) to be resolved based on 178 syntenic blocks. The calculated genomic distance between the genomes reflects the age of divergence between the B and A/C genome lineages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Fig6">6a</a>). While <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> have chromosomes sharing extensive homology with ancestral chromosomes, the extent of the rearrangements separating the B genome would explain the limited genic exchange that has been possible across the two lineages. Therefore, capturing new diversity from the third <i>Brassica</i> genome for crop improvement strategies in its related species may be more efficient using next-generation breeding techniques such as clustered regularly interspaced short palindromic repeat/Cas9. The defined block relationships between the genomes also provide further avenues for studying centromere evolution because, among the 27 centromeric regions across the three species, 26 had adjacent or flanking conserved blocks found across either two or three of the genomes, suggesting evolutionary conserved positions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">22</a>; compare A8, C8 and B7).</p><p>The ability to generate, relatively quickly and affordably, contiguous genome assemblies provides a platform for the development of true pan-genomes for many species. Such assemblies will allow an accurate comparison of not only gene content, but also repeat composition and distribution, and reveal the range and complexity of structural variation. There are still some limitations, and assemblies of neopolyploid genomes will need to be assessed to determine whether the technology can routinely differentiate young WGD events. However, with continuing improvements to the technology and optimization of software dedicated to analyses of these new data types, resolution of these problems should be swift.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Plant materials and DNA extraction</h3><p><i>Brassica nigra</i><a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> (C2) and Ni100 were grown in a greenhouse at Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada, Saskatoon Research and Development Centre, under 20/18 °C, 16/8-h days. Leaf tissue was collected from 3-week-old plants after 2 d of dark treatment, flash-frozen and stored at −70 °C. Nuclear isolation was performed as described in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Zhang, H. B., Zhao, X., Ding, X., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wing, R. A. Preparation of megabase‐size DNA from plant nuclei. Plant J.7, 175–184 (1995)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2476">51</a></sup>, and high-molecular-weight DNA was extracted using a modified cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Allen, G., Flores-Vergara, M., Krasynanski, S., Kumar, S. &amp; Thompson, W. J. Np A modified protocol for rapid DNA isolation from plant tissues using cetyltrimethylammonium bromide. Nat. Protoc.1, 2320–2325 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2480">52</a></sup>. Briefly, approximately 20 g of leaf tissue was homogenized in 200 ml of ice-cold 1× Hanks’ buffered salt (HBS) solution (0.01 M Trizma base, 0.08 M KCL, 1 mM spermidine, 0.01 M EDTA, 0.5 M sucrose, 1 mM spermine plus 0.15% β-mercaptoethanol). Five millilitres of 1× HBS plus 20% Triton X-100 was added to the homogenate and mixed slowly with a magnetic stir bar for 1 h on ice, then filtered through two layers of cheesecloth and one layer of Miracloth. The nuclei were pelleted by centrifugation of homogenate at 1,800<i>g</i> for 20 min at 4 °C. The pellet was washed by resuspension in 1× HBS plus 0.5% Triton ×100 on ice and centrifuged three times at 1,800<i>g</i> for 20 min at 4 °C to purify the nuclei. The final pelleted nuclei were resuspended in 10 ml of lysis buffer (100 mM TrisHCl, 100 mM NaCl, 50 mM EDTA, 2% CTAB) treated with proteinase K, followed by RNAase A (37 °C for 30 min), and high-molecular-weight DNA was extracted after two cycles of phenol/chloroform clean-up and ethanol precipitation. DNA quality and quantity were measured using an Agilent Bioanalyzer and Qubit fluorometer, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">ONT sequencing and reads processing</h3><p>The C2 genome was sequenced on a MinION while the Ni100 genome was sequenced on a GridION. For the C2 genome, 1D (SQK-LSK108) and 1D<sup>2</sup> (SQK-LSK308) genomic DNA libraries were prepared following the nanopore protocol (<a href="https://community.nanoporetech.com/protocols">https://community.nanoporetech.com/protocols</a>). For size-selected DNA, 4 µg of DNA was sheared with a Covaris g-TUBE to obtain 10-kb fragments. Two micrograms of sheared and un-sheared DNA was used for library preparation for both the 1D and 1D<sup>2</sup> methods. For the Ni100 genome, 1D (SQK-LSK109) and Rapid (SQK-RAD004) libraries were prepared for sequencing on GridION. MinION sequencing used MinKnow v.1.4.2 with albacore (v.1.1.2) live base calling, enabled with default parameters. ONT reads with read quality score ≥10 (q10) were filtered from the ONT fastq files (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">3</a>). For the Ni100 genome, sequenced using GridION, MinKnow 2.0 and live base calling was completed with Guppy, and ONT reads with read quality score ≥7 (q7) were used for assembly. Nanostat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="De Coster, W., D’Hert, S., Schultz, D. T., Cruts, M. &amp; Van Broeckhoven, C. NanoPack: visualizing and processing long-read sequencing data. Bioinformatics34, 2666–2669 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2512">53</a></sup> was used to compute the sequencing statistics for each run with both raw and quality-filtered data.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Illumina sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA extracted as above was used for whole-genome Illumina sequencing. For <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> (C2), 2 µg of DNA was fragmented using a Covaris sonicator to obtain 350-bp fragments, and a TruSeq DNA PCR-Free library was prepared following the manufacturer’s protocol (Illumina, Inc.). The normalized library was paired-end sequenced in 2 × 101 bp and 2 × 250 bp rapid-run mode on the HiSeq 2500 platform (Illumina, Inc.). In total, &gt;82 Gb of SR sequences with ~137× physical coverage were generated for C2 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">28</a>). For Ni100, whole-genome shotgun Illumina paired-end (300–700 bp insert size) and Illumina and Roche/454 (Life Sciences) mate-pair libraries (3–45 kb insert size) were developed following the manufacturers’ protocols. In total 115 Gb (~192× physical coverage) were sequenced and used for whole-genome assembly by SOAPdenovo (v.1.05) following a previously documented approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome Biol.15, R77 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2534">18</a></sup> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">28</a>).</p><p>Total RNA was extracted from bud, flower, leaf, seedling, root and silique tissue samples for Ni100, and from leaf and bud samples for C2, using the RNeasy plant mini kit (QIAGEN), including on-column DNase digestion (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">28</a>). Total RNA integrity and quantity were assessed on a Bioanalyzer (Agilent). Illumina TruSeq RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) libraries were prepared, and 125-bp, paired-end sequencing was performed using the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. A total of 11 and 39 Gb raw Illumina RNA-seq data were generated for C2 and Ni100, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">28</a>). Reads were filtered for low quality (&lt;q30), adaptor sequence, potential PCR duplicates and length (&lt;55 bp) with Trimmomatic (v.0.32). RSEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. J. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinformatics12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2553">54</a></sup> (rsem-calculate-expression) was used to calculate expression, in transcripts per million.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genome size estimation based on <i>k</i>-mer analysis</h3><p>Jellyfish v.2.2.6 was used to estimate <i>k</i>-mer frequency distribution based on the subset (~35 Gb) of raw 2 × 250 PE Illumina reads with a <i>k</i>-mer length of 17. The output histogram was uploaded to findGSE to estimate genome size, heterozygosity and repeat fraction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Sun, H., Ding, J., Piednoël, M. &amp; Schneeberger, K. findGSE: estimating genome size variation within human and Arabidopsis using k-mer frequencies. Bioinformatics34, 550–557 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2575">55</a></sup>. Analysis has shown that genome size is about 570 and 607.8 Mb for Ni100 and C2, respectively, and was used as a haploid genome size for the study (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">25</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Nanopore sequence assembly and polishing</h3><p>Raw ONT fastq data were filtered for quality at q10 and q7 for C2 and Ni100, respectively, and the resulting reads were error corrected using CANU 1.6 with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Koren, S. et al. CANU: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res.27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2591">24</a></sup>. The C2 filtered data were assembled with three different assemblers (SMARTDenovo, wtdbg, Miniasm). Minimap2 was used to generate overlaps of corrected reads, with <i>k</i> = 24 and other default parameters (-csw5 -L100 -m0) followed by assembly using miniasm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Koren, S. et al. CANU: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res.27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2598">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Li, H. Minimap and miniasm: fast mapping and de novo assembly for noisy long sequences. Bioinformatics32, 2103–2110 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2601">56</a></sup>. SMARTDenovo (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo">https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo</a>) was used with <i>k</i> = 24 and recommended parameters. The wtdbg tool (<a href="https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg">https://github.com/ruanjue/wtdbg</a>) was used to assemble the reads, with <i>k</i> = 17, <i>k</i> = 24 and default parameters (-H -S 1.02 -e 3). The best assembly for the C2 genome (S4), based on contiguity and genome coverage, was selected for further analysis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">1</a>). Based on this preliminary analysis, the Ni100 genome were assembled using SMARTDenovo with <i>k</i>-mer 24 and default parameters. Both draft assemblies were polished using eight iterations of PILON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2635">25</a></sup> with available Illumina reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Contig scaffolding</h3><p>Leaf tissue from C2 was provided to Dovetail genomics (Santa Cruz), who prepared and sequenced CHiCAGO and Hi-C libraries. The polished assemblies, CHiCAGO and Dovetail Hi-C library reads were used as input for scaffolding using Dovetail’s HiRise pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Moll, K. M. et al. Strategies for optimizing BioNano and Dovetail explored through a second reference quality assembly for the legume model, Medicago truncatula. BMC Genomics18, 578 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2647">57</a></sup>. A modified SNAP read mapper uses CHiCAGO and Hi-C reads to align to the draft assembly, while HiRise produces a likelihood model for the genomic distance between read pairs, computing the optimum threshold to join contigs and identify putative misjoins.</p><p>A genetic map derived from genotyping-by-sequencing data of a back-cross population of 72 <i>B. nigra</i> lines, derived from the Ni100 × double-haploid line A1//Ni100, was used to anchor contigs from all assemblies to the pseudo-molecules. In total, 20,689, 19,666 and 21,034 loci were anchored to the genome assemblies of C2, Ni100-SR and Ni100-LR, respectively. The assembly was confirmed using genome-ordered graphical genotypes (GOGGs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="He, Z. &amp; Bancroft, I. J. Organization of the genome sequence of the polyploid crop species Brassica juncea. Nat. Genet.50, 1496–1497 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2657">58</a></sup> based on transcriptome re-sequencing of lines from the <i>Brassica juncea</i> VHDH mapping population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Ramchiary, N. et al. Mapping of yield influencing QTL in Brassica juncea: implications for breeding of a major oilseed crop of dryland areas. Theor. Appl. Genet.115, 807–817 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2664">59</a></sup> and genome re-sequencing of lines from the <i>B. juncea</i> YWDH population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Guo, S. et al. A genetic linkage map of Brassica carinata constructed with a doubled haploid population. Theor. Appl. Genet.125, 1113–1124 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2672">60</a></sup>. GOGGs also enabled the incorporation of four previously unanchored scaffolds into the chromosome assemblies. Sequences of restriction fragment length polymorphism clones used to generate the genetic map in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Lagercrantz, U. &amp; Lydiate, D. J. J. RFLP mapping in Brassica nigra indicates differing recombination rates in male and female meioses. Genome38, 255–264 (1995)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2676">61</a></sup> were aligned to the assemblies to name and orient the pseudo-molecules accordingly, based on the internationally agreed standard (<a href="http://www.Brassica.info">http://www.Brassica.info</a>). A look-up table comparing chromosome (linkage group) names between the two published nomenclatures for the B genome is shown in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">6</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Assembly quality assessment</h3><p>Quality of the assembly was estimated using single-copy orthologous gene analysis (BUSCO v.0.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2698">26</a></sup> with Embryophyta OrthoDB v.9. The 1,440 genes were searched in the assembly using Augustus (v.3.2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics19, 215–225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2702">62</a></sup>, NCBI’s BLAST (v.2.2.31+)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2706">63</a></sup> and HMMER (v.3.1b2) by BUSCO. In addition, genome discrepancies were estimated using qualimap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Okonechnikov, K., Conesa, A. &amp; García-Alcalde, F. Qualimap 2: advanced multi-sample quality control for high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics32, 292–294 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2710">27</a></sup> by mapping Illumina reads against the polished assembly. Bowtie-2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2714">64</a></sup>) with default parameters was used for mapping of Illumina reads against the assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome annotation</h3><p>RNA-sequencing (39 Gb) data for Ni100 and C2 were aligned against their respective genome assemblies using STAR v.2.7 (maximum 3% mismatches over 95% read length), and subsequently assembled using the Trinity (v.2.8.4) genome-guided approach with default parameters. In total, 110,767 and 124,851 transcripts were assembled for Ni100 and C2, respectively. The assembled transcripts, along with protein sequences from <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>, were used as evidence for the MAKER-P annotation pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Campbell, M. S., Holt, C., Moore, B. &amp; Yandell, M. Genome annotation and curation using MAKER and MAKER‐P. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics48, 4.11.1–4.11.1.39 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2738">65</a></sup>. Snap and Augustus ab initio predictors were configured for use by MAKER-P in hint-based mode, using protein and transcript as input evidence. Approximately 6% of the predicted gene models were found to be misjoined based on <i>A. thaliana</i> gene structure and <i>B. nigra</i> transcript evidence, and were split into two or more alternate models. PASA (v.2.3.3) software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res.31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2749">66</a></sup> was then used to further assemble Trinity output and to incorporate the transcript alignment evidence into MAKER gene annotation. In total, 59,877 and 67,030 coding genes were annotated for Ni100 and C2, respectively. Of the annotated genes, 48,621 (81.2%) of Ni100 and 54,586 (81.4%) of C2 gene models have expression values of transcripts per million (TPM) &gt; 0. BLASTP revealed that 55,022 (92.0%) of Ni100 and 59,780 (89.2%) of C2 genes have significant hits (cut-off 10 × 10<sup>–5</sup>) against the Uniprot plant database.</p><p>The gene-naming convention proposed for <i>B. rapa</i> v.3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, L. et al. Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Hortic. Res.5, 50 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2761">21</a></sup>) was used with minor modifications: Bni (for <i>B. nigra</i>) followed by the chromosome number with leading zero, and the letter ‘g’ (for gene)—for example, B01g (for B genome chromosome 1). Six-digit gene numbers were assigned in steps of ten, with leading zeros from top to bottom of chromosomes. Following the gene number and separated by a period, to distinguish genome versions and between genotypes, ‘2N’ was assigned to Ni100 LR (genome version 2) and ‘1C2’ to C2 (genome version 1)—for example, BniB01g023500.2N. Low-confidence genes were defined as those models with neither transcriptome evidence support nor significant hits to the Uniprot plant database. Low-confidence genes were named similarly as described above but with the letter ‘p’ to distinguish them.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Repeat annotation</h3><p>A de novo repeat library was developed using RepeatModeler (v.1.0.11; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>), which uses two de novo repeat-finding programmes (RECON and RepeatScout) for identification of repeat families. After removal of potential false positives based on homology with <i>A. thaliana</i> gene models, a total of 374 repeat models were retained. In addition, a previously developed repeat library for <i>B. nigra</i> Ni100, which contains 950 repetitive elements, was merged to develop a final repeat library with 1,324 elements that was used for repeat annotation in the whole genome. Repeatmasker was employed to estimate repeat copies, proportion and distribution into the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Tarailo‐Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. J. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics25, 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2789">67</a></sup>.</p><p>Centromeric location was identified based on the distribution of centromere-associated repeats such as CRB, <i>B. nigra-</i>specific centromere-associated repeat (pBN35—<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/X16588.1">X16588.1</a>) and CENH3-associated sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Lim, K. B. et al. Characterization of the centromere and peri-centromere retrotransposons in Brassica rapa and their distribution in related Brassica species. Plant J.49, 173–183 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2806">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Schelfhout, C. J., Snowdon, R., Cowling, W. A. &amp; Wroth, J. M. A PCR based B-genome-specific marker in Brassica species. Theor. Appl. Genet.109, 917–921 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2809">68</a></sup>. Based on the distribution of these elements using BLAST, centromere regions were located in the assembly.</p><p>Full-length, long terminal repeat retrotransposons were identified from both genome assemblies using LTR_harvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2816">69</a></sup> and LTR_Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res.35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2820">70</a></sup>. The resulting outputs (.scn) were fed into the LTR retriever programme<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol.176, 1410–1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2824">71</a></sup> to extract FL-LTR-RTs. Copy number, distribution and divergence time of LTR-RTs were comparatively analysed between the two reference genomes. FL-LTR-RTs were classified into different families based on homology with the repeat library from Repeat Explorer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Neumann, P., Novák, P., Hoštáková, N. &amp; Macas, J. Systematic survey of plant LTR-retrotransposons elucidates phylogenetic relationships of their polyprotein domains and provides a reference for element classification. Mobile DNA10, 1 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2828">72</a></sup>. Potentially conserved FL-LTR-RTs were identified through reciprocal BLAST analyses of the unique flanking DNA sequences, when paired flanking DNA was positioned syntenically and within 5 Mb of the position in the alternate genome they were considered conserved. The complexity of the <i>B. nigra</i> centromere regions was represented by a graphical network formed from an adjacency matrix of FL-LTR-RTs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">19b,c</a>). The matrix was determined from sequential pairing of annotated TE elements present in the centromeric or pericentromeric definitions. All analyses were performed using R, and the graphical representation made using the igraph package.</p><p>Phylogenetic analysis was done using the reverse-transcriptase (RT) domain sequences of ALE and OTA elements from the three <i>B. nigra</i> genomes. RT domains obtained from the Pfam database (accession nos. PF07727 and PF00078) were used as a query to search against the FL-LTRs of ALE and OTA sequences, respectively, by BLASTx, and the best hit with a minimum of 200-bp overlap with query sequences was used for further analysis. RT domain sequences of ALE and OTA families from the three <i>B. nigra</i> genomes were aligned separately by the clustalW aligner, and a tree was generated using the neighbour-joining method with 500 bootstrap replications by MEGA7. FL-LTR-RTs annotated for the whole genome using the LTR retriever were manually analysed to identify nested TE insertion.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Gene and genome evolution</h3><p>OrthoFinder v.2.2.7 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. J. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol.16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2856">31</a></sup>) was used to identify members of gene families and assess their expansion in C2 and Ni100-LR, by clustering annotated genes with the closely related species <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, L. et al. Improved Brassica rapa reference genome by single-molecule sequencing and chromosome conformation capture technologies. Hortic. Res.5, 50 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2862">21</a></sup>, <i>B. oleracea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome Biol.15, R77 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2868">18</a></sup> and <i>A. thaliana</i> (Araport11) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">11</a>). CAFE v.4.2.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. J. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol.30, 1987–1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2879">32</a></sup>) was used to identify expansion, contraction and rapidly evolving gene families among the six genomes based on the orthogroups obtained from OrthoFinder analysis.</p><p>Synteny analysis was performed to identify syntenic genes between <i>B. nigra</i> Ni100-LR/C2 and <i>A. thaliana</i> using the <i>A. thaliana</i> proteome (Araport10) as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome Biol.15, R77 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2895">18</a></sup>. Briefly, based on best BLASTP values (1 × 10<sup>–20</sup> or better), syntenic gene pairs between <i>B. nigra</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> were employed in DAGChainer with default parameters to compute the chain score<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Haas, B. J., Delcher, A. L., Wortman, J. R. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2908">73</a></sup>. Manual curation based on better chain score was done to create the final syntelog table (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">22</a>). Tandemly duplicated and proximal genes were identified following an approach previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Qiao, X. et al. Gene duplication and evolution in recurring polyploidization–diploidization cycles in plants. Genome Biol.20, 38 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2915">74</a></sup>. Briefly, potential homologous pairs between each of three genomes were identified by all-versus-all BLASTP at 1 × 10<sup>–10</sup>. MCScanX (default parameters) was then used to identify duplicated pairs from Ni100 and C2 that formed intra-species syntenic chains. These pairs were set aside and classified as WGD-derived gene pairs. The remaining pairs (or BLASTP hits) were classified as either tandem (adjacent to each other on the same chromosome) or proximal (&gt;1 and ≤10 genes on the same chromosome).</p><p>For phylogenetic analysis, a data matrix consisting of 1,150 syntelogs retained in <i>A. thaliana</i> and each of the subgenomes (least fractionated, LF; most fractionated 1, MF1; most fractionated 2, MF2) of <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. nigra</i> was constructed. Sequences from individual syntelog gene sets were aligned using ClustalW v.2.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Larkin, M. A. et al. Clustal W and Clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics23, 2947–2948 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2936">75</a></sup>), and poorly aligned regions were removed using trimAL v.1.2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Capella-Gutierrez, S., Silla-Martinez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldon, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2941">76</a></sup>). Trimmed sequences were concatenated using the Phyutility programme<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Smith, S. A. &amp; Dunn, C. W. Phyutility: a phyloinformatics tool for trees, alignments and molecular data. Bioinformatics24, 715–716 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2945">77</a></sup> to produce the final data matrix comprising a total alignment length of 807,943 bp. Phylogenetic relationships were inferred using the maximum-likelihood method implemented in RAxML v.8.2.12 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2949">78</a></sup>), using rapid bootstrapping (100 replications) and a GTRGAMMA substitution model. The resulting phylogenetic tree was visualized using the interactive Tree of Life v.4 web server<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive Tree Of Life (iTOL) v4: recent updates and new developments. Nucleic Acids Res.47, W256–W259 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2953">79</a></sup>.</p><p>In the DCJ model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Yancopoulos, S., Attie, O. &amp; Friedberg, R. J. B. Efficient sorting of genomic permutations by translocation, inversion and block interchange. Bioinformatics21, 3340–3346 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d44283e2960">42</a></sup>, two genomes being compared are represented as a ‘breakpoint’ graph allowing calculation of the genomic distance and DCJ distance between genomes. For ancestral genome reconstruction, given gene orders in a set of genomes <i>G</i> and a distance measure <i>d</i>, the median problem is to find an ancestral genome (<i>m</i>) that minimizes the sum of distances: <span class="mathjax-tex">\(d^{\mathrm{\Sigma }} = d({{m,{{g}})}},\forall{{{g}} \in G}\)</span>. The median problem is known to be NP-hard under the DCJ distance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Tannier, E., Zheng, C. &amp; Sankoff, D. J. Multichromosomal median and halving problems under different genomic distances. BMC Bioinformatics10, 120 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3037">80</a></sup>. Given three genomes and <i>d</i><sub>i,j</sub> as the pairwise DCJ distance between genomes i and j, the metric <span class="mathjax-tex">\(d^{\mathrm{\Sigma }}\)</span> has the following properties—the lower bound is <span class="mathjax-tex">\(d_{\mathrm{l}}^{\mathrm{\Sigma }} = \frac{{d_{1,2} + d_{2,3} + d_{1,3}}}{2}\)</span> and the upper bound is <span class="mathjax-tex">\(d_{\mathrm{u}}^{\mathrm{\Sigma }} = d_{1,2} + d_{2,3} + d_{1,3} - {\mathrm{max}}\{ d_{1,2},d_{2,3},d_{1,3}\}\)</span><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Xu, A. W. DCJ median problems on linear multichromosomal genomes: graph representation and fast exact solutions. In RECOMB International Workshop on Comparative Genomics (Eds Ciccarelli, F. D. &amp; Miklós, I.) 70–83 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3345">81</a></sup>. Therefore, given the pairwise DCJ distances calculated for the three genomes, <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. nigra</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>, <span class="mathjax-tex">\(d_{\mathrm{l}}^{\mathrm{\Sigma }} = 123\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\(d_{\mathrm{u}}^{\mathrm{\Sigma }} = 148\)</span>. The ASMedian-linear algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Xu, A. W. DCJ median problems on linear multichromosomal genomes: graph representation and fast exact solutions. In RECOMB International Workshop on Comparative Genomics (Eds Ciccarelli, F. D. &amp; Miklós, I.) 70–83 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3443">81</a></sup> is designed to find exact solutions to the DCJ median problem on multi-chromosomal genomes. It uses a divide-and-conquer approach to decompose the multiple breakpoint graph to its ‘adequate’ subgraphs, find optimal solutions for its parts and then combine the optimal solutions. A total of 25,866 orthologous genes were identified between the genomes of <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. nigra</i> and <i>B. oleracea.</i> These genes were used to identify 178 unique syntenic blocks where, although distances between blocks were estimated based on fractionated genes, block reversals were used to fix block breakpoints. Finally, an ancestral genome was calculated using the ASMedian-linear algorithm as nine ancestral chromosomes with a genome size of 321 Mb. The calculated ancestral genome (<i>m</i>) under the DCJ model generates a minimum total DCJ distance: <span class="mathjax-tex">\({\it{d}}^{\mathrm{\Sigma }} = 124\)</span>, where <i>d</i><sub><i>m,</i></sub><sub>rapa</sub> = 26, <i>d</i><sub><i>m,</i></sub><sub>nigra</sub> = 70 and <i>d</i><sub><i>m,</i></sub><sub>oleracea</sub> = 28.</p><p>Distribution of synonymous substitutions (Ks) was performed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kagale, S. et al. Polyploid evolution of the Brassicaceae during the Cenozoic era. Plant Cell26, 2777–2791 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3522">14</a></sup>. Briefly, for each pair of syntelogs between the <i>Brassica</i> species or the subgenomes of each <i>Brassica</i> species, protein sequences were aligned using ClustalW v.2.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Koo, D. H. et al. Rapid divergence of repetitive DNAs in Brassica relatives. Genomics97, 173–185 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3532">7</a></sup>) and the corresponding codon alignments were produced using PAL2NAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res.34, W609–W612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3536">82</a></sup>. Ks values for each sequence pair were calculated using the maximum-likelihood method implemented in codeml of the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol.24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3541">83</a></sup> under the F3x4 model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Goldman, N. &amp; Yang, Z. A codon-based model of nucleotide substitution for protein-coding DNA sequences. Mol. Biol. Evol.11, 725–736 (1994)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3545">84</a></sup>. Histograms were generated using log-transformed Ks &gt; 0.001. Gaussian mixture models were fitted to the ln(Ks) values using the R package Mclust, and the number of Gaussian components, the mean of each component and fractions of data were calculated. The Bayesian information criterion was used to determine the best-fitting model to the data. The fit of the determined models was confirmed by <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> tests.</p><p>The presence of resistance genes was identified using the RGAugury pipeline (v.2017.10.21)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Li, P. et al. RGAugury: a pipeline for genome-wide prediction of resistance gene analogs (RGAs) in plants. BMC Genomics17, 852 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3556">85</a></sup>; transcription factors, transcription regulators and protein-kinase families were identified by iTAK (current v.1.7, 13 May 2016)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Zheng, Y. et al. iTAK: a program for genome-wide prediction and classification of plant transcription factors, transcriptional regulators, and protein kinases. Mol. Plant9, 1667–1670 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3560">86</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Structural variant analysis</h3><p>Structural variants such as insertions, deletions, inversions, duplications and translocations were identified using both Ni100-LR and C2-LR assemblies. Raw long reads of both genomes were mapped using NGMLR LR aligner on Ni100-LR as a reference, and SVs were called using Sniffles with a minimum read depth of 20 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Sedlazeck, F. J. et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat. Methods15, 461–468 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3573">33</a></sup>). Likewise, SVs were predicted using C2-LR as a reference assembly. Furthermore, cross-validation of SVs identified by Sniffles was done using another SV identifier, Picky, with the same read depth of 20 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gong, L. et al. Picky comprehensively detects high-resolution structural variants in nanopore long reads. Nat. Methods15, 455–460 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3577">34</a></sup>). SVs shared by both callers were identified as high-quality SVs and used for further analysis. At least 15 random SVs of each type were assayed manually, and suggested almost 100% prediction accuracy for deletions and insertions; however, some of the larger predicted events appeared less reliable and it was apparent that a number of deletions had been overlooked, suggesting that the criteria may have been too stringent. Seven SVs caused by repeats were manually validated by gel analysis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">29</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">WGBS</h3><p>Genomic DNA was isolated from leaf tissue of <i>B. nigra</i> Ni100 with two biological replications, and from leaf and bud tissue from <i>B. nigra</i><a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CN115125">CN115125</a> nuclei, using QIAGEN’s DNeasy Plant Kit following the manufacturer’s protocol. A Zymo Research EZ DNA Methylation kit was used for bisulfite conversion on 100 ng of DNA, along with 0.5% w/w unmethylated lambda DNA (Promega), included to evaluate bisulfite conversion efficiency. Library construction was performed according to the Illumina TruSeq DNA Methylation Kit Reference Guide (no. 15066014, v.01). The libraries were quantified as above and paired-end sequenced (2 × 125 bp) using an Illumina HiSeq 2000.</p><p>Quality-filtered WGBS reads were used to analyse cytosine methylation ratios following alignment using BSMAP (v.2.9) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM3">28</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Xi, Y. &amp; Li, W. BSMAP: whole genome bisulfite sequence MAPping program. BMC Bioinformatics10, 232 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3610">87</a></sup>. Lambda DNA was included in each library as a control to estimate bisulfite conversion efficiency. In all instances the conversion rate was estimated at &gt;99%. The evidence to assign the methylatation status of each cytosine surveyed was determined using binomial probability distribution. Methylation patterns were determined and summarized using the support from available genome annotation. Methylation patterns were partitioned by context (CG, CHH, CHG), reflecting the underlying biochemistry underpinning their maintenance. Statistical relationships and data organization were performed using custom Perl and R scripts, with support from Datatable, dplyr, stringr, genomation and MethylKit; all graphical summaries were developed using ggplot2. CpG islands were identified by EMBOSS using the cpgplot tool with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet.16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3614">88</a></sup>. These features were filtered based on position to include only those residing in the 5′ regulator region (−2,000 to 0 bp from ATG) of the annotated gene features. DNA methylation detected using the ONT and WGBS methods was compared at each cytosine occurring in the CG context throughout the filtered islands. Agreement between methylation base calls was assessed at individual loci, and the similarity represented graphically following dimension reduction (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">CpG context in nanopore reads by Nanopolish</h3><p>Because nanopore reads have the facility to output signals for both methylated and unmethylated cytosine bases, Nanopolish was used to detect the CpG context in the whole genome of Ni100 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Simpson, J. T. et al. Detecting DNA cytosine methylation using nanopore sequencing. Nat. Methods14, 407–410 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d44283e3629">35</a></sup>). Nanopolish v.0.10.1 was used to call bases for both methylated and unmethylated bases from raw nanopore reads, and results were filtered as described based on either log-likelihood ratio or read depth.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>All genome assembly and annotation-associated DNA-seq, RNA-seq and WGBS-seq data have been deposited with NCBI under BioProject ID <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA516907">PRJNA516907</a>. The assembled pseudo-molecules and associated annotation files, along with a Jbrowse instance for each genome, can be accessed at <a href="http://cruciferseq.ca">http://cruciferseq.ca</a>. All supporting data are included in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0735-y#Sec20">Source data</a> are provided with this paper.</p>

S.P. and E.E.H. performed ONT sequencing. L.T. and Z.-K.N. developed the back-cross mapping population and linkage map. Z.H. and I.B. carried out additional genetic confirmation of scaffold/contig order. C.S.K., S.P., L.J. M.B. and I.A.P.P. carried out assembly and bioinformatic and statistical analyses. S.K. performed Ks-based divergence analysis. L.J., C.Z. and D.S. performed ancestral genome characterization. K.N.H. and S.J.R. performed bisulfite sequencing and methylome analysis. B.C. provided additional RNA-seq data. S.P. and I.A.P.P. wrote the manuscript. All authors read and contributed to the final manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

                    class="c-article-further-reading__authors-list js-list-authors-3 js-etal-collapsed" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"
                    id="authors-3">
                  <li><span
                        class="js-separator"></span><span>Mathieu Rousseau-Gueutin</span></li><li><span

              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-3">
                <i>GigaScience</i>
